<script>
  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_073ec58b-93ea-41ad-89d7-260e02e84f6f'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }
  //]]>
</script>

<span id="freeTextContainer13340332341983894633">How can anyone describe this book? It isn't a parable, a fairy story, or a poem, but rather a mixture of all three. It is beautiful and it is comic. It is philosophical and it is cheery.   <span id="freeText13340332341983894633" style="display:none">How can anyone describe this book? It isn't a parable, a fairy story, or a poem, but rather a mixture of all three. It is beautiful and it is comic. It is philosophical and it is cheery. What we suppose we are trying fumblingly to say is, in a word, that it is Thurber.<br /><br />There are only a few reasons why everybody has always wanted to read this kind of story: if you have always wanted to love a Princess; if you always wanted to be a Prince; if you always wanted the wicked Duke to be punished; or if you always wanted to live happily ever after. Too little of this kind of thing is going on in the world today. But all of it is going on valorously in <i>The 13 Clocks</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13340332341983894633" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/238438-does-anybody-know-how-to-contact-goodreads">Does anybody know how to contact Goodreads directly, to complain about a (this) book&#39;s blurb? I am deeply put off by its assumption that the world is inhabited only by straight men (&quot;Everybody has always wanted to love a Princess. Everybody has always wanted to be a Prince&quot;). It makes me not want to read this book, which is unfair to the book&#39;s author.</a>
</div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1176987919" />
    <a title="Fabian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1036893-fabian"><img alt="Fabian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514061293p2/1036893.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1176987919?book_show_action=true">Jan 22, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fabian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fabian" href="/user/show/1036893-fabian">Fabian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1176987919" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5305294757437278080">Beyond the absurdity of the Shrek universe (satire, satire, product placement, nostalgic nod, satire), this one actually pays tribute to those acts of chivalry so prevalent in fairy tales and children's books. It is witty, playful, but also deep and adult. It has, as one ogre who really gets on my nerves would say, many layers, just like an onion does.<br/><br/>This is better than "Le Petit Prince" GASP!; rather more in tune with my (if I do have any; I guess I know that I do) American proclivities, than </span>
  <span id="freeText5305294757437278080" style="display:none">Beyond the absurdity of the Shrek universe (satire, satire, product placement, nostalgic nod, satire), this one actually pays tribute to those acts of chivalry so prevalent in fairy tales and children's books. It is witty, playful, but also deep and adult. It has, as one ogre who really gets on my nerves would say, many layers, just like an onion does.<br /><br />This is better than "Le Petit Prince" GASP!; rather more in tune with my (if I do have any; I guess I know that I do) American proclivities, than that other Children's night-time story. This one's heroic and valiant, that other melancholic and existential: but this one has great strings of laughs, &amp; a more strikingly animated imagination, and it has brilliant sections, &amp; it is, yep, too short, for my liking. Its sole mistake is not reserving more time for the reader to take in the freshness &amp; experience the whimsy that borders on that singular Wonderlandesque feeling people who like the idea of Lewis Carroll's Alice, though not the haphazard &amp; random stories themselves, wish they possessed.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5305294757437278080" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_33456090" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/33456090" />
    <a title="RandomAnthony" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/721021-randomanthony"><img alt="RandomAnthony" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1470919631p2/721021.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/33456090?book_show_action=true">Sep 21, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="RandomAnthony" class="user" itemprop="url" name="RandomAnthony" href="/user/show/721021-randomanthony">RandomAnthony</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer33456090" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10520328499076938653">Oh my god, "The 13 Clocks" is genius. How did this book stay off my radar for so long? Who can I blame? I only heard of the book because Neil Gaiman wrote an introduction (I think) to a new edition in which he highly praises "13 Clocks". I ordered a copy from the library (an older copy, without the Gaiman introduction...our library system doesn't have the new edition yet) and I read the entire text in about an hour, maybe a little less. "13 Clocks" reads like a lovely meld of "The Phantom Tollbo</span>
  <span id="freeText10520328499076938653" style="display:none">Oh my god, "The 13 Clocks" is genius. How did this book stay off my radar for so long? Who can I blame? I only heard of the book because Neil Gaiman wrote an introduction (I think) to a new edition in which he highly praises "13 Clocks". I ordered a copy from the library (an older copy, without the Gaiman introduction...our library system doesn't have the new edition yet) and I read the entire text in about an hour, maybe a little less. "13 Clocks" reads like a lovely meld of "The Phantom Tollbooth" and Gaiman's novels; in fact, Thurber's influence on Gaiman is startling. The book is dark, like much of Gaiman's work, and Thurber clearly thinks younger readers can handle a little blood and guts. But the book is packed with funny and inventive passages and Thurber's flawless storytelling shines through on every page. <br /><br />For example...here's a brief, spoiler-free passage:<br /><br /><i>The Duke limped because his legs were of different lengths. The right one had otugrown the left because, when he was young, he had spent his mornings place-kicking pups and punting kittens. He would say to a suitor, "What is the difference in the length of my legs?" and if the youth replied, "Why, one is shorter than the other," the Duke would run him through with the sword he carried in his swordcane and feed him to the geese. The suitor was supposed to say, "Why, one is longer than the other." Many a prince had been run through for naming the wrong difference. Others had been slain for offenses equally trivial; trampling the Duke's camllias, failing to praise his wines, staring too long at his gloves, gazing too long at his niece. Those who survived his scorn and sword were given incredible labors to perform in order to win his niece's hand, the only warm hand in the castle, where time had frozen to death at ten minutes to five one snowy night. They were told to cut a slice of moon, or change the ocean into wine. They were set to finding things that never were, and building things that could not be. They came and tried and failed and disappeared and never came again. And some, as I have said, were slain, for using names that start X, or dropping spoons, or wearing rings, or speaking disrespectfully of sin."</i><br /><br />Brilliant. If you want to remember why some books aimed at younger readers pulse with joy and energy, check out "The 13 Clocks." I'm buying the new edition ASAP.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10520328499076938653" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_413213885" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/413213885" />
    <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely"><img alt="K.D. Absolutely" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1471967140p2/2156200.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/413213885?book_show_action=true">Sep 12, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="user" itemprop="url" name="K.D. Absolutely" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely">K.D. Absolutely</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to K.D. by: </span>
      1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die (2006-2012)
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=1001-core">1001-core</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=childrens">childrens</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer413213885" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10710093771316296035">I picked up this book thinking that this could be classified as just another children's book. It does have many fairy tale ingredients yet it uses metaphors similar to those of Antoine de Saint-Exupery in his unforgettable classic, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/157993.The_Little_Prince' title='The Little Prince by Antoine de Saint-Exupéry' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Little Prince</a> (3 stars). Some of the metaphors used by St-Ex easily escaped me but most of them I was able to relate to my personal experiences. Same is true here with James Thurber's 1950 fantasy tale, <i>The 13 Clocks.</i> <br/><br/>The story is about an evil <b>Duke</b> who has been c</span>
  <span id="freeText10710093771316296035" style="display:none">I picked up this book thinking that this could be classified as just another children's book. It does have many fairy tale ingredients yet it uses metaphors similar to those of Antoine de Saint-Exupery in his unforgettable classic, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/157993.The_Little_Prince" title="The Little Prince by Antoine de Saint-Exupéry" rel="nofollow noopener">The Little Prince</a> (3 stars). Some of the metaphors used by St-Ex easily escaped me but most of them I was able to relate to my personal experiences. Same is true here with James Thurber's 1950 fantasy tale, <i>The 13 Clocks.</i> <br /><br />The story is about an evil <b>Duke</b> who has been cursed together with his kingdom. It is too cold everywhere. This cold has frozen the hands of the 13 clocks in the palace. The only warm hands belong to his nice <b>Saralinda</b> who is so beautiful that she is adored by many princes around the world. However, his "uncle" the evil Duke would make all impossible demands to these princes. Upon failing the demands, the Duke would knife the princes and feed their bodies to the geese. Then came <b>Prince Zorn of Zorna</b> whole alias is <b>Xingu</b> whose is able to trick the Duke with the help of <b>Golux.</b> Golux brings Prince Zorn of Zorna to a woman called <b>Hangga</b> whose tears turn magically to precious gems.<br /><br />The plot seems like an outright fairy tale, right? Yes and no. Yes, because the events are all make-believe. No, because the story is not told in a fashion that ordinary children would easily grasp. For example, the way Golux speaks is kind of riddle-like similar to how Gollum in LOTR would deliver his lines. This can be confusing even to a grownup like me. There are also some scenes that can be disturbing to children like the feeding of the human flesh to the geese, the kidnapping of Saralinda and the disappearance of the Duke at the end of the story can give a child a nightmare if there is no comforting adult to tell or explain the story to a small child.<br /><br />As a grown up, though, I really enjoyed reading this book. It is also accompanied by colorful illustrations that made my reading quite memorable. I like the hat that is <i>indescribable.</i> It is really. Check this book out and see for yourself.<br /><br />I am looking forward to reading more James Thurber now.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10710093771316296035" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2315523748" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Cheryl" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3498889-cheryl"><img alt="Cheryl" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590863781p2/3498889.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2315523748?book_show_action=true">Mar 03, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cheryl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cheryl" href="/user/show/3498889-cheryl">Cheryl</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2315523748" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2689109481426749759">I've read this several times and want to again, after seeing that Neil Gaiman and I actually agree on this tight masterpiece of word-play and adventure and satire.... <br/><br/>(In case you don't know, I want to like Gaiman's work, but I don't.)<br/>........<br/>Another reread.<br/><br/> I really need a box set of Thurber's juveniles. I get so much more out of them every time. There's a mystery, and satire, drama, wordplay, and a layer of Thurberian melancholy under it all, providing a resonance, in all four of these fables.</span>
  <span id="freeText2689109481426749759" style="display:none">I've read this several times and want to again, after seeing that Neil Gaiman and I actually agree on this tight masterpiece of word-play and adventure and satire.... <br /><br />(In case you don't know, I want to like Gaiman's work, but I don't.)<br />........<br />Another reread.<br /><br /> I really need a box set of Thurber's juveniles. I get so much more out of them every time. There's a mystery, and satire, drama, wordplay, and a layer of Thurberian melancholy under it all, providing a resonance, in all four of these fables.<br /><br />I should reread <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/19785034.The_Secret_Life_of_Walter_Mitty" title="The Secret Life of Walter Mitty by James Thurber" rel="nofollow noopener">The Secret Life of Walter Mitty</a> and see how similar to these it is.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2689109481426749759" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12568927065928073572"><i>"Once upon a time, in a gloomy castle on a lonely hill, where there were thirteen clocks that wouldn't go, there lived a cold, aggressive Duke, and his niece, the Princess Saralinda..."</i><br/><br/>This was a fast and fun romp. One part wicked to two parts whimsy, it's a book to delight all ages. Children will love the story, and adults will enjoy the more sophisticated humor and word play. If you know anything about James Thurber, (or maybe even if you don't), you'll appreciate how much fun he had writing t</span>
  <span id="freeText12568927065928073572" style="display:none"><i>"Once upon a time, in a gloomy castle on a lonely hill, where there were thirteen clocks that wouldn't go, there lived a cold, aggressive Duke, and his niece, the Princess Saralinda..."</i><br /><br />This was a fast and fun romp. One part wicked to two parts whimsy, it's a book to delight all ages. Children will love the story, and adults will enjoy the more sophisticated humor and word play. If you know anything about James Thurber, (or maybe even if you don't), you'll appreciate how much fun he had writing this story. In the Foreword he explains that he wrote <i>The Thirteen Clocks</i> when he was supposed to be working on another book. He called this "an example of escapism and self-indulgence." Sixty years later, it served the same purpose for me. <br /><br />The funky and stylized artwork is entertaining, too. When Zorn of Zorna dons his princely raiment, he looks like he's wearing a frilly tablecloth! But it's a welcome change from the uniformity of today's computerized graphics. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12568927065928073572" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_33465583" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/33465583" />
    <a title="Valerie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/543625-valerie"><img alt="Valerie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1233245342p2/543625.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/33465583?book_show_action=true">Sep 21, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Valerie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Valerie" href="/user/show/543625-valerie">Valerie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781556111884" href="/book/show/490120.The_13_Clocks">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer33465583&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer33465583" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2457404549208651048">In <u>The Thirteen Clocks</u>, a prince (disguised as a minstrel) attempts to win the hand of Saralinda, the niece of an evil Duke who keeps her prisoner while killing most of the suitors who try to win her hand by setting impossible tasks for them to accomplish. A crazy little guy (called the Golux) decides to help the prince in his quest.<br/><br/>I liked this book, but here's what I got stuck on: Why did the Golux help this prince, and not the other ones? When it came down to it, the prince didn't really figu</span>
  <span id="freeText2457404549208651048" style="display:none">In <u>The Thirteen Clocks</u>, a prince (disguised as a minstrel) attempts to win the hand of Saralinda, the niece of an evil Duke who keeps her prisoner while killing most of the suitors who try to win her hand by setting impossible tasks for them to accomplish. A crazy little guy (called the Golux) decides to help the prince in his quest.<br /><br />I liked this book, but here's what I got stuck on: Why did the Golux help this prince, and not the other ones? When it came down to it, the prince didn't really figure anything out. The Golux pretty much spoon-fed him everything he should do, and helped him figure everything out.<br /><br />I'm not so sure that this was a prince worthy enough to win the hand of Saralinda. I think she should've gone off with the Golux at the end.<br /><br />What's wrong with me? Everyone loves this book. Did I miss something? Or do I just hate traditional fairy tales where the prince rescues the princess (even though he's a total dumbass?)<br /><br />Should I have read something else by Thurber before this?<br /><br />I did understand and appreciate the wordplay in the book, but that was about it. I enjoyed reading it, but don't understand what all the fuss is about. I mean, I could probably have rescued the princess myself with the Golux by my side.<br /><br />That prince dude bugged me. Up with the Golux!</span>
  <a data-text-id="2457404549208651048" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer240009741" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5064218375775125757">This was a wonderful, farcical Children's fairy tale that I would never have come across had it not been on Boxall's 1001 Books to Read Before You Die list. Reading this book reminded me of when I happily read Dr. Seuss to my children, not really knowing who loved the books more, who giggled the most, who said "again", just glowing in that feel good emotion that only sharing the best children's books with them brought out in me. How we missed this one is beyond me, I feel as though I've short-ch</span>
  <span id="freeText5064218375775125757" style="display:none">This was a wonderful, farcical Children's fairy tale that I would never have come across had it not been on Boxall's 1001 Books to Read Before You Die list. Reading this book reminded me of when I happily read Dr. Seuss to my children, not really knowing who loved the books more, who giggled the most, who said "again", just glowing in that feel good emotion that only sharing the best children's books with them brought out in me. How we missed this one is beyond me, I feel as though I've short-changed them.<br /><br />I'm not exactly sure why the book was on this particular list, but I enjoyed it nonetheless and will doubtless read it again. As an added bonus, this edition came with an introduction by Neil Gaiman, which is a treat in itself. <br /><br />To rate it is difficult, what do I compare it to, other children's books, most of which I've forgotten, or just on its own? I guess I'll go with a combination of the two and hope I don't lead anyone astray.</span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4674014-marquise?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4674014-marquise?shelf=what-a-weird-world">what-a-weird-world</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4674014-marquise?shelf=oh-the-twists">oh-the-twists</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4674014-marquise?shelf=charming-story">charming-story</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4674014-marquise?shelf=bonus-points-for-creativity">bonus-points-for-creativity</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4674014-marquise?shelf=novellas">novellas</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2279795730" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12343148250488352387">Second Thurber I read in my life, and this time I loved the book way more than <i>Walter Mitty</i>, the first of his stories I read. This is a very weird fairy tale-esque Fantasy story, with an absurd plotline that's meant to make you laugh, and writing that's very convoluted and full of wordplay and silly rhymes. Good for a quick read when one's in the mood for a bit of unpretentious humour. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_251877443" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/251877443" />
    <a title="D.M. Dutcher " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5208457-d-m-dutcher"><img alt="D.M. Dutcher " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1338694848p2/5208457.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/251877443?book_show_action=true">Dec 27, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="D.M. Dutcher " class="user" itemprop="url" name="D.M. Dutcher " href="/user/show/5208457-d-m-dutcher">D.M. Dutcher </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5208457-d-m-dutcher?shelf=bad-novels">bad-novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5208457-d-m-dutcher?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5208457-d-m-dutcher?shelf=overrated">overrated</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer251877443" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12930562843329561341">I don't get the hype.<br/><br/>Look, this was released by Dell Yearling, so theoretically it is a children's novel. Children do not know who James Thurber is, do not usually care about whether or not a story is philosophical or Thurberian, and while they do enjoy wordplay and fun, aren't placing as high a value on it as the sheer delight a story involves. Seeing this on the back cover was my first warning, and the book confirms my right to be.<br/><br/>The writing is like a sword that is so encrusted with brilliant</span>
  <span id="freeText12930562843329561341" style="display:none">I don't get the hype.<br /><br />Look, this was released by Dell Yearling, so theoretically it is a children's novel. Children do not know who James Thurber is, do not usually care about whether or not a story is philosophical or Thurberian, and while they do enjoy wordplay and fun, aren't placing as high a value on it as the sheer delight a story involves. Seeing this on the back cover was my first warning, and the book confirms my right to be.<br /><br />The writing is like a sword that is so encrusted with brilliant gold, jewels, and finery that you can't actually swing the thing. This is a fairy tale where the writing gets in the way of the tale. It's simply too self-conscious. There's too many knowing winks, descriptions which are cutesy and go on a bit too long for the story, but are finely written and have an exquisite turn of phrase. I can deal with ornate prose in the service of a story: this is the only reason why <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1474546.The_Worm_Ouroboros" title="The Worm Ouroboros by E.R. Eddison" rel="nofollow noopener">The Worm Ouroboros</a> works for many people. But the fussiness and the precise, chiseled writing focuses attention on it, and the tale bored me to tears.<br /><br />For a book which uses a similar idea, but works tremendously, Eleanor Estes's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/42366.The_Witch_Family" title="The Witch Family by Eleanor Estes" rel="nofollow noopener">The Witch Family</a> uses the same mechanics of made-up words and surreal situations, but makes it work with memorable characters, no sense of irony or tongue in cheek whatsoever, and evocative settings and plot as well as language. Or if you want how to make a tale that works without it, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/36638.The_Wolves_of_Willoughby_Chase" title="The Wolves of Willoughby Chase by Joan Aiken" rel="nofollow noopener">The Wolves of Willoughby Chase</a> shows how you can use functional, unadorned prose and excellent detail to evoke a far away time and place. Both of these books are small masterpieces of kidlit, much more deserving than this one.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12930562843329561341" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText9419811275411957557" style="display:none"><br><i>It's always Then. It's never Now.</i><br><br>Time, for children, just never moves fast enough.     <a title="Anne Blocker" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/388993-anne-blocker"><img alt="Anne Blocker" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1190139894p2/388993.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/6365262?book_show_action=true">Sep 17, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anne Blocker" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anne Blocker" href="/user/show/388993-anne-blocker">Anne Blocker</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/388993-anne-blocker?shelf=strong-influence">strong-influence</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6365262" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3527732509052818915">Carolyn Cantwell introduced me to this book. She was a concert pianist headed for law school, majoring in American literature. I was a pre-med student fascinated with technology, dissecting fetal pigs in the kitchen. I loved poetry, folk and rock and didn't read books. I looked things up and read the funny papers. I challenged her to find a book that could hold my attention to the end. She took on my cutural development as a project and gave me Thirteen Clocks.<br/><br/>I enjoyed the pictures and set it a</span>
  <span id="freeText3527732509052818915" style="display:none">Carolyn Cantwell introduced me to this book. She was a concert pianist headed for law school, majoring in American literature. I was a pre-med student fascinated with technology, dissecting fetal pigs in the kitchen. I loved poetry, folk and rock and didn't read books. I looked things up and read the funny papers. I challenged her to find a book that could hold my attention to the end. She took on my cutural development as a project and gave me Thirteen Clocks.<br /><br />I enjoyed the pictures and set it aside. Then one night while I was building a color television set, she fixed herself a martini, turned off my music, picked up the book and read it aloud. I was hooked. Guggle to zatch.<br /><br />I don't know how many times I have read it -- to myself and aloud to others. I always enjoy it. The next book on her list was The Little Prince.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_9321380" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/9321380" />
    <a title="Matthew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/630182-matthew"><img alt="Matthew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1195510590p2/630182.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/9321380?book_show_action=true">Nov 19, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matthew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matthew" href="/user/show/630182-matthew">Matthew</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">everyone</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer9321380" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5759408068878555417">James Thurber's The Thirteen Clocks is an allegorical fairy tale for adults that primarily showcases Thurber's wit and mischievous wordplay. Although it's ostensibly about an evil Duke who keeps his niece, the Princess Saralinda locked in his cold dark castle, where time has been stopped by his own sword, and feeds her potential suitors to his geese, I don't recommend it as a bedtime story to your six-year-old unless you want them to turn out like me. Thanks dad! I mean that. The 13 Clocks is si</span>
  <span id="freeText5759408068878555417" style="display:none">James Thurber's The Thirteen Clocks is an allegorical fairy tale for adults that primarily showcases Thurber's wit and mischievous wordplay. Although it's ostensibly about an evil Duke who keeps his niece, the Princess Saralinda locked in his cold dark castle, where time has been stopped by his own sword, and feeds her potential suitors to his geese, I don't recommend it as a bedtime story to your six-year-old unless you want them to turn out like me. Thanks dad! I mean that. The 13 Clocks is simply one of the most brilliant tales ever spun. The Princess can only be saved by a prince who's name begins with X, the Golux wears an "indescribable hat" and, by his own admission is the only Golux, and not a Mere Device. The Dungeon is inhabited by the Todal, an unseen horror that "feels as though it's made of lip and makes the sound of rabbits screaming." This, like the great WB cartoons of the '30s is perfect entertainment for the young, yet is fraught with puns and puzzles aimed squarely at the well-read. The only people who won't like it are those dried up fools who've lost their sense of wonder. Hopefully that's not you. <br /><br />"Hark hark, the dogs do bark, the beggars are coming to town,<br />Some in rags and some in tags and some in velvet gowns.<br />Hark hark the dogs to bark, the duke is fond of kittens,<br />he likes to take their insides out and use their fur for mittens."<br /><br />(from memory)<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5759408068878555417" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_914284658" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/914284658" />
    <a title="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2190636-joselito-honestly-and-brilliantly"><img alt="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349701607p2/2190636.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" href="/user/show/2190636-joselito-honestly-and-brilliantly">Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer914284658" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2445321880275492341">"An American Classic" the book cover declares. And I agree. For here, one can find many things which America had made famous:<br/><br/>1.Kidnapping - the Princess Saralinda wasn't really the Duke's niece. He kidnapped her when she was little;<br/><br/>2. Vicious Murders - the Duke had killed, and gutted like fish, countless victims and fed their flesh to his geese;<br/><br/>3. Lust - the Duke lusted after Saralinda and was not able to immediately consummate his evil desire only because of a witch's spell (a case of evil susp</span>
  <span id="freeText2445321880275492341" style="display:none">"An American Classic" the book cover declares. And I agree. For here, one can find many things which America had made famous:<br /><br />1.Kidnapping - the Princess Saralinda wasn't really the Duke's niece. He kidnapped her when she was little;<br /><br />2. Vicious Murders - the Duke had killed, and gutted like fish, countless victims and fed their flesh to his geese;<br /><br />3. Lust - the Duke lusted after Saralinda and was not able to immediately consummate his evil desire only because of a witch's spell (a case of evil suspending another evil);<br /><br />4. Marriage based merely on lust - Prince Zorn and Princess Saralinda, without any time to even talk, hang out, go out on a date or get to know each other --and just because they're both good-looking--marched immediately off to get married;<br /><br />5. Materialism - lots of emphasis here on gems and jewels and the message that the rich (Prince) marrying the rich (Princess) is something that goes SOP and need not have any thoughtful deliberation;<br /><br />6. Espionage - recall the CIA snooping on people, even those non-Americans abroad;<br /><br />7. Betrayal - the Duke's spies turning out to be traitors;<br /><br />8. Doublespeak - words are used not in the aid of truth but to confuse, deceive and provide meaningless entertainment; and <br /><br />9. Sorcery and Witchcraft - the setting is a godless place. The message is the triumph of vice over vice.<br /><br /><br />First published in 1950, I am sure none of the American kids who grew up with this has turned up to be a saint.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_45760549" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/45760549" />
    <a title="Brenton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/177581-brenton"><img alt="Brenton" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1184786794p2/177581.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/45760549?book_show_action=true">Feb 08, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brenton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brenton" href="/user/show/177581-brenton">Brenton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781590172759" href="/book/show/2093969.The_13_Clocks">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/177581-brenton?shelf=fantasy-and-adventure">fantasy-and-adventure</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer45760549" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1032786425305006461">This is certainly a fantasy book from the 50s. It reminds me very much of all the old 50s children books that were in my grandparents' house in California, old books that had been my mother's when she was a little girl. This is partly so because of the illustrations by Marc Simont, who's style is very much like just about any other children's book from the 40s and 50s that I remember reading. In fact, I'm sure he illustrated at least a handful of those very books I remember from my visits to Gra</span>
  <span id="freeText1032786425305006461" style="display:none">This is certainly a fantasy book from the 50s. It reminds me very much of all the old 50s children books that were in my grandparents' house in California, old books that had been my mother's when she was a little girl. This is partly so because of the illustrations by Marc Simont, who's style is very much like just about any other children's book from the 40s and 50s that I remember reading. In fact, I'm sure he illustrated at least a handful of those very books I remember from my visits to Grandma and Grandad's; the bio in the back of The Thirteen Clocks says he illustrated nearly 100 in his career.<br /><br />The writing of James Thurber also makes me think of other fifty and sixty year old children's books I've read, because the story bounces along from one non sequitur or deus ex machina to another in a completely carefree manner, not concerned so much with rhyme and reason as it is with being fun and adventuresome. It's true fantasy - things are the odd way they are because, well, in the world of The Thirteen Clocks, that's just <i>the way things are!</i><br /><br />For example, the story does not concern itself with who the Golux is or where he came from or what his true motives in helping the hero prince are - it is enough that we, the readers, know that the Golux is a funny little man with a strange hat and even stranger things to say and that of course he would know things that are secret and appear and disappear at will, or otherwise he wouldn't deservedly have a title as odd as 'the Golux'! I'm sure that the Golux makes perfect sense to any child who's read the book with half an imagination, and that strikes me as something that Neil Gaiman would write - so it should be no surprise to you that he wrote the introduction to this pretty little hardcover reprinting.<br /><br />None of this means that The Thirteen Clocks is fluffy and harmless, fit for Puritan ears; there is the expectedly evil Duke who is, indeed, <i>evil</i>, and there are dark nights with lightning and thunder and an unseen creature here and there waiting to gobble up hapless heroes-to-be. What it does mean is that this book is not heavy and involving. There is a time and place for fantasy works with rich, intricate, worlds and myths and histories laced with their own cogent reason and meaning. There is also a time and place for fantasy that is jaunty and dream-like, and when that mood strikes you, read The Thirteen Clocks.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1032786425305006461" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText14288307109161059946" style="display:none">i picked this up in an independent book shop, knowing full well it wasn't really my thing. neil gaimain calls the 13 clocks, "probably the best book in the world" and that alone kind of solidified my feeling that i probably wouldn't like it. aaaand i was right.<br /><br />it's not so much that it was a bad book. it's just...for a certain crowd. if you like neil gaiman, or monty python or the princess bride and stuff like that, you will most likely enjoy this book. i don't much care for any of the aforementioned (with the exception of the graveyard book and coraline, because those i truly enjoyed). <br /><br />this book contained a creepy castle, a psychopathic duke, a beautiful princess, and a prince disguised as a minstrel, attempting to win the princess's hand in marriage by completing what are essentially 'impossible tasks.' <br /><br />there are rhymes, puns, clever little idiosyncratic sentences, but it didn't really do anything for me. and the illustrations kind of creeped me out. soooo. yeah. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11405541442403044689">The blurb on my copy of this book described it as not a "parable, fairy tale, or poem, but rather a mix of all three." In fact, it achieves none. And the duke, the ultimate representation of evil in this parable, is as flat as the rest of them, a slimy fellow who limps around threatening to slay people from their zatch to their guggle.   <span id="freeText11405541442403044689" style="display:none">The blurb on my copy of this book described it as not a "parable, fairy tale, or poem, but rather a mix of all three." In fact, it achieves none. We are told the princess is good, but we are given no proof why. We are told the prince is good, but he seems nothing more than a spoiled rich kid. And the duke, the ultimate representation of evil in this parable, is as flat as the rest of them, a slimy fellow who limps around threatening to slay people from their zatch to their guggle. Humorous, occasionally, yes, and evil, yes, but this is not the sort of evil most of us know. <br />And the plot is no better, as our one-dimensional characters run around tripping over plot holes. We are constantly told about spells witches cast over various characters to explain their many inconsistencies, but never throughout the book is a witches spell used, and there are plenty of opportunities. The Golux can magically appear in the prince's dungeon cell, but he must trudge through brambles for days to reach a destination. The princess's previous suitors were given tasks like traveling thrice around the moon, but our hero is conveniently tasked with bringing a thousand jewels. Difficult, yes. Impossible, no. <br />Besides for all that, this book is a perfect example of ridiculous American culture. The princess never speaks once throughout the book other than the words "I wish him well." And yet the prince risks his life over and over again to marry her because she is beautiful. The duke is evil because he slays his enemies, but we must rejoice when, in the end, he is killed by a creature more evil than him. This is not a story of good triumphing over evil but of the rich and beautiful triumphing over the rich and ugly. <br />Here, though, I must acknowledge Thurber's wonderful alliterations and word plays, many of which made me laugh out loud. This book, if nothing else, is funny. <br />Two stars for the whimsical language. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11405541442403044689" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<span id="freeTextContainer16050146604959515934">If you love cleverly written fantasy books, it doesn't get much better than the 13 Clocks. The story takes you on a wild and yet slightly farcical ride as you follow the prince's efforts to win the hand of the princess from her wicked Uncle. Yes, this book has the classic elements of a fairy tale but it's also filled with imaginative interpretations of everyday things such as the Duke who killed time (an event which left blood on his sleeves). This is a children's book and a short read, but well</span>
  <span id="freeText16050146604959515934" style="display:none">If you love cleverly written fantasy books, it doesn't get much better than the 13 Clocks. The story takes you on a wild and yet slightly farcical ride as you follow the prince's efforts to win the hand of the princess from her wicked Uncle. Yes, this book has the classic elements of a fairy tale but it's also filled with imaginative interpretations of everyday things such as the Duke who killed time (an event which left blood on his sleeves). This is a children's book and a short read, but well worth the time.</span>

<div id="review_2449887412" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2449887412" />
    <a title="Ed Erwin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1411444-ed-erwin"><img alt="Ed Erwin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604431321p2/1411444.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2449887412?book_show_action=true">Jul 08, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ed Erwin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ed Erwin" href="/user/show/1411444-ed-erwin">Ed Erwin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143110149" href="/book/show/29661916-the-13-clocks">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2449887412" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13565465102328332926">This story does not need an introduction, says Neil Gaiman in his introduction. He's right. And I hope someday to read a book that does not have a contribution from Neil Gaiman. (Seriously, he's everywhere right now!) But I'm happy to have his introduction here. He says: "It was filled with words. And while all books are filled with words, this one was different: it was filled with magical, wonderful, tasty words. It slipped into poetry and out of it again in a way that made you want to read it </span>
  <span id="freeText13565465102328332926" style="display:none">This story does not need an introduction, says Neil Gaiman in his introduction. He's right. And I hope someday to read a book that does not have a contribution from Neil Gaiman. (Seriously, he's everywhere right now!) But I'm happy to have his introduction here. He says: "It was filled with words. And while all books are filled with words, this one was different: it was filled with magical, wonderful, tasty words. It slipped into poetry and out of it again in a way that made you want to read it aloud, just to see how it sounded."<br /><br />This is basically a whimsical little fairy story. But it shows such joy at playing with words. It is prose, but like Gaiman said, it slips into poetry and out again at the drop of a hat. Occasionally a limerick will be printed as a poem, but mostly you'll just be reading a paragraph of prose and suddenly realize that the last few lines were a perfect rhyming couplet. Elsewhere, you'll find lots of assonance and alliteration. No two characters have names beginning with the same letter, allowing different alliterative games with each character's name.<br /><br />Such a delight should not be read only by children.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13565465102328332926" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

		<td>
		    <b>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Kt4nr3QNDawdeEuvXrnvj62dgIW4QwWZsMOz3+q99ms+f5OEWtoGFK+Y4T5Pq+1XCTIlz+ZZ8N3yvFOLpd3N1A==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ycpgibSDu4X2hAyQIsyLDdajXozSpvAwPQk5oBmtNM3da9SimlSwPURkpgEz3onVcgz7xoy8BXR/dtn0Vs0Pcg==')})
  }

Saunders Lewis\'s first novel was fiercely attacked by Welsh-speaking critics for its portrayal of sexual obsession and manipulation. For the first time the psychology which was such a feature of the work of contemporary authors was brought into Welsh fiction. And for the first time sex, prostitutes and venereal diseases were given prominence in a Welsh novel. Despite outraged accusations of immorality, Monica is a novel with deep moral foundations, describing as it does the short-comings of a relationships based on infatuation and physical passion.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"15184713049493594333\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div class=\'wtrButtonContainer       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/16839.James_Thurber">About James Thurber</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/16839.James_Thurber"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1183238729p3/16839.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/16839.James_Thurber">James Thurber
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
457
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer3695459210060749483">Thurber was born in Columbus, Ohio to Charles L. Thurber and Mary Agnes (Mame) Fisher Thurber. Both of his parents greatly influenced his work. His father, a sporadically employed clerk and minor politician who dreamed of being a lawyer or an actor, is said to have been the inspiration for the small, timid protagonist typical of many of his stories. Thurber described his mother as a "born comedien</span>
  <span id="freeText3695459210060749483" style="display:none">Thurber was born in Columbus, Ohio to Charles L. Thurber and Mary Agnes (Mame) Fisher Thurber. Both of his parents greatly influenced his work. His father, a sporadically employed clerk and minor politician who dreamed of being a lawyer or an actor, is said to have been the inspiration for the small, timid protagonist typical of many of his stories. Thurber described his mother as a "born comedienne" and "one of the finest comic talents I think I have ever known." She was a practical joker, on one occasion pretending to be crippled and attending a faith healer revival, only to jump up and proclaim herself healed.<br /><br />Thurber had two brothers, William and Robert. Once, while playing a game of William Tell, his brother William shot James in the eye with an arrow. Because of the lack of medical technology, Thurber lost his eye. This injury would later cause him to be almost entirely blind. During his childhood he was unable to participate in sports and activities because of his injury, and instead developed a creative imagination, which he shared in his writings.<br /><br />From 1913 to 1918, Thurber attended The Ohio State University, where he was a member of the Phi Kappa Psi Fraternity. He never graduated from the University because his poor eyesight prevented him from taking a mandatory ROTC course. In 1995 he was posthumously awarded a degree.<br /><br />From 1918 to 1920, at the close of World War I, Thurber worked as a code clerk for the Department of State, first in Washington, D.C. and then at the American Embassy in Paris, France. After this Thurber returned to Columbus, where he began his writing career as a reporter for the Columbus Dispatch from 1921 to 1924. During part of this time, he reviewed current books, films, and plays in a weekly column called "Credos and Curios," a title that later would be given to a posthumous collection of his work. Thurber also returned to Paris in this period, where he wrote for the Chicago Tribune and other newspapers.<br /><br />In 1925, he moved to Greenwich Village in New York City, getting a job as a reporter for the New York Evening Post. He joined the staff of The New Yorker in 1927 as an editor with the help of his friend and fellow New Yorker contributor, E.B. White. His career as a cartoonist began in 1930 when White found some of Thurber's drawings in a trash can and submitted them for publication. Thurber would contribute both his writings and his drawings to The New Yorker until the 1950s.<br /><br />Thurber was married twice. In 1922, Thurber married Althea Adams. The marriage was troubled and ended in divorce in May 1935. Adams gave Thurber his only child, his daughter Rosemary. Thurber remarried in June, 1935 to Helen Wismer. His second marriage lasted until he died in 1961, at the age of 66, due to complications from pneumonia, which followed upon a stroke suffered at his home. His last words, aside from the repeated word "God," were "God bless... God damn," according to Helen Thurber.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3695459210060749483" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
